arXiv:2604.14409v1 [hep-ph] 15 Apr 2026
Astrophysical bounds on the high-energy evolution of neutrino mixing
Mauricio Bustamante
mbustamante@nbi.ku.dk
Niels Bohr International Academy, Niels Bohr Institute,
University of Copenhagen, 2100 Copenhagen, Denmark
Qinrui Liu
qinrui_liu@sfu.ca
Department of Physics, Simon Fraser University, Burnaby, BC V5A 1S6, Canada
Arthur B. McDonald Canadian Astroparticle Physics Research Institute, Kingston ON K7L 3N6, Canada
Gabriela Barenboim
gabriela.barenboim@uv.es
Departament de Física Teórica and IFIC, Universitat de València-CSIC, E-46100, Burjassot, Spain
(April 15, 2026)
Abstract
While conventional oscillation experiments measure neutrino mixing parameters with high precision, these measurements are strictly confined to sub-TeV scales. At higher energies, renormalization-group effects can cause these parameters to evolve with the transferred momentum,
Q
Q
. High-energy and ultra-high-energy astrophysical neutrinos, spanning TeV to EeV energies, probe high values of
Q
Q
unreachable by conventional experiments, offering an unprecedented test of high-energy mixing. We use the flavor composition of these neutrinos—the relative proportions of
ν
e
\nu_{e}
,
ν
μ
\nu_{\mu}
, and
ν
τ
\nu_{\tau}
—to constrain this evolution, both phenomenologically and within dimension-6 Standard Model Effective Field Theory. We account for astrophysical uncertainties—an unavoidable requirement to obtain realistic results, even though this weakens the bounds. Although present IceCube measurements lack the sensitivity to detect this running, we forecast that upcoming multi-detector combinations will place unprecedented bounds on the high-energy evolution of neutrino mixing.
I
Introduction
Neutrino oscillations—the periodic transformation of one neutrino flavor into another during propagation—are measured with high precision. Combined observations of solar, atmospheric, reactor, and accelerator neutrinos have established that mixing among the three active flavors—
ν
e
\nu_{e}
,
ν
μ
\nu_{\mu}
, and
ν
τ
\nu_{\tau}
—is described by the Pontecorvo–Maki–Nakagawa–Sakata (PMNS) matrix
[
1
,
2
]
, parametrized by three mixing angles
(
θ
12
,
θ
23
,
θ
13
)
(\theta_{12},\theta_{23},\theta_{13})
and a CP-violation phase (
δ
CP
\delta_{\rm CP}
). Today, the mixing angles are known to a precision of 1–3% [at 68% confidence level (C.L.)] and the CP-violation phase, to about 16%
[
3
,
4
,
5
]
.
Yet, the values of the mixing parameters are inferred exclusively from sub-TeV neutrino experiments (see, however, Ref.
[
6
]
). Thus, a fundamental question remains:
are the mixing parameters universal constants, or are they different at low and high neutrino energies?
The expectation from quantum field theory is that, indeed, the parameters evolve—or
run
—with the transferred momentum,
Q
Q
, at which neutrino interactions occur, the value of which rises with energy (as is the case for dimensionless couplings). However, no evolution has been observed to date.
Significant evolution of the mixing parameters would modify the
ν
α
→
ν
β
\nu_{\alpha}\to\nu_{\beta}
oscillation probabilities (
α
,
β
=
e
,
μ
,
τ
\alpha,\beta=e,\mu,\tau
) relative to their standard, no-running expectation, leading to potentially detectable effects in neutrino experiments capable of distinguishing flavors. While the running—and its effect on the probabilities—is expected to grow more prominent with
Q
Q
, accessing these values requires higher-energy neutrinos, which are scarcer.
From theory, the predicted size and form of the running of the mixing parameters are model-dependent. In the Standard Model (SM) extended with massive Majorana neutrinos, the mass matrix responsible for granting neutrinos mass is generated by the dimension-5 Weinberg operator
[
7
]
. Its Wilson coefficients—and hence the physical mixing parameters—evolve with
Q
Q
through renormalization group equations (RGEs)
[
8
,
9
]
. Within the SM, however, the running is negligible, since it is suppressed by the small tau Yukawa coupling,
y
τ
≈
0.01
y_{\tau}\approx 0.01
. But in other well-motivated extensions, such as the Minimal Supersymmetric Standard Model (MSSM)
[
10
,
11
,
12
,
13
]
or, more generally, the SM Effective Field Theory (SMEFT) supplemented with higher-dimensional operators
[
14
,
15
,
16
,
17
]
, the running can be significantly enhanced, potentially shifting the mixing parameters by observationally relevant amounts between low- and high-energy scales.
Thus, detecting appreciable RG running of the neutrino mixing parameters would constitute unmistakable evidence of new physics.
From experiment, sub-TeV neutrino data have probed primarily momenta
Q
≲
Q\lesssim
15 GeV. Reference
[
18
]
(see also Refs.
[
19
,
20
,
21
]
) showed that, in this regime, extending the SM with a new, light secluded sector could induce observable effects in present (T2K, NO
ν
\nu
A) and future long-baseline GeV-scale neutrino experiments, including new sources of CP violation, zero-baseline flavor transitions, and apparent CPT violation. However, if the characteristic energy scale of the RG-inducing new physics is instead heavy—
i.e.
, above the TeV scale—the RG running of the mixing parameters would be undetectable in conventional oscillation experiments.
In such case, higher neutrino energies would be needed to access higher momenta. Above the TeV scale, neutrinos are detected via their deep inelastic scattering (DIS) off nucleons
[
22
,
23
,
24
]
. In DIS, a neutrino of energy
E
ν
E_{\nu}
can transfer, as a maximum, a momentum of
2
​
E
ν
​
m
N
\sqrt{2E_{\nu}m_{N}}
, where
m
N
≈
1
m_{N}\approx 1
GeV is the nucleon mass. For instance, atmospheric neutrinos with energies of up to
E
ν
∼
E_{\nu}\sim
100 TeV may probe momenta as high as 450 GeV. In reality, however, the
average
value of
Q
Q
in the DIS of 100-TeV neutrinos is significantly smaller—around 7 GeV—because the parton distribution functions (PDFs) of the nucleon favor lower values of
Q
Q
(more precisely, lower values of the Bjorken-
x
x
parameter).
To overcome this, we turn to even higher-energy neutrinos of astrophysical origin—the most energetic ones known, with TeV–EeV energies. They provide a unique window into the high-
Q
Q
regime—as proposed by Ref.
[
25
]
and then revisited by Refs.
[
18
,
26
]
—and into new physics in general
[
27
,
28
,
29
,
30
,
31
,
32
]
. High-energy astrophysical neutrinos with energies in the TeV–10 PeV range are detected regularly by neutrino telescopes IceCube
[
33
]
, KM3NeT
[
34
]
, and Baikal-GVD
[
35
]
, soon to be joined by more detectors
[
30
,
32
,
36
]
. Ultra-high-energy (UHE) neutrinos above 100 PeV will be targeted by next-generation dedicated telescopes
[
30
,
32
,
36
]
, while the first UHE neutrino has already been observed by KM3NeT
[
37
]
.
The most energetic among these neutrinos may access momenta of tens of TeV. Yet, as for atmospheric neutrinos, the PDFs pull the average
Q
Q
down. This, combined with the increasing scarcity of neutrinos of growing energy, places the average
Q
Q
accessible by high- and ultra-high-energy astrophysical neutrinos at 20–40 GeV (Fig.
1
). This range represents roughly an order-of-magnitude improvement in the average
Q
Q
available to probe the high-energy evolution of neutrino mixing.
II
Synopsis
We probe the RG running of neutrino mixing by searching for its imprint on the
flavor composition
of high-energy astrophysical neutrinos
—the relative fractions of
ν
e
\nu_{e}
,
ν
μ
\nu_{\mu}
, and
ν
τ
\nu_{\tau}
arriving at Earth
[
38
,
39
,
40
,
41
]
—which reflects RG-induced changes in the flavor-transition probabilities. The flavor composition is a versatile probe of astrophysics
[
38
,
42
,
43
,
44
,
45
,
46
,
39
,
47

,

,
56
,
57
,
58
,
41
,
59
,
60
,
61
,
62
,
63
]
and fundamental physics
[
64
,
44
,
45
,
65
,
66
,
67
,
47
,
49
,
68
,
50
,
51
,

,

,

,

,
41
,
59
,
31
,
32
,
62
,
96
,
97
]
. While short terrestrial baselines restrict conventional oscillation experiments primarily to two-flavor transitions, astrophysical neutrinos undergo full three-flavor mixing over cosmological distances, enabling tests of this paradigm
[
66
,
98
,

,
67
,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,

,
50
,

,
111
,
77
,
6
]
.

.

in the deep inelastic scattering of high-energy (TeV–PeV) and ultra-high-energy (UHE,

. Present measurements reflect the NuFIT 6.1 global oscillation fit

]
. Projections are for TeV–PeV multi-detector observations and UHE detection in the radio array of IceCube-Gen2, assuming astrophysical neutrino production via full or muon-damped pion decay. Evolution under a specific SMEFT RG running scheme is shown for illustration (Sec.
IV.5
).
We execute a two-pronged analysis: first, treating the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters
(
θ
12
′
,
θ
23
′
,
θ
13
′
,
δ
CP
′
)
(\theta_{12}^{\prime},\theta_{23}^{\prime},\theta_{13}^{\prime},\delta_{\rm CP}^{\prime})
as free phenomenological quantities to remain agnostic to specific RG scenarios; and second, constraining RG-inducing dimension-6 SMEFT coefficients.
Figure
1
summarizes our main results. We adopt present IceCube TeV–PeV flavor measurements
[
112
]
and project the sensitivity of multi-detector combinations. Present data offer no sensitivity, but
future detectors will constrain
θ
23
′
\theta_{23}^{\prime}
and
θ
13
′
\theta_{13}^{\prime}
by 2040.
While our projections yield limited precision compared to conventional oscillation experiments—owing primarily to the unknown flavor composition with which neutrinos are produced at their sources—they are meaningful. Extending observations to the UHE regime yields weaker constraints due to larger flavor uncertainties.
The rest of this paper is organized as follows. Section
III
introduces the formalism of standard and RG-modified flavor transitions, the production of high-energy astrophysical neutrinos, and our predictions for their flavor composition at Earth. Section
IV
details the RG running of neutrino mixing parameters, with a focus on the SMEFT and the momentum distributions accessible to neutrino telescopes. Section
V
outlines our statistical procedure used to constrain both the generic high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters and the specific SMEFT coefficients. Section
VI
presents our main results, including present limits, future TeV–PeV multi-detector combinations, and UHE radio array projections. Finally, Section
VII
summarizes our findings and outlook. Appendices
A
–
H
contain additional derivations and detailed results.
III
Flavor composition in high-energy astrophysical neutrinos
We establish the theoretical and experimental framework for predicting and measuring the flavor composition of high-energy astrophysical neutrinos. We detail how the standard PMNS matrix maps the initial flavor composition at the source—canonically produced via full or muon-damped pion decay—to the observable flavor composition at Earth. We introduce the phenomenological framework for modifying these flavor-transition probabilities to account for potential high-
Q
Q
renormalization-group running at the detection scale. By juxtaposing these theoretical predictions against the detection capabilities of current and next-generation neutrino telescopes, we demonstrate the premise of this work: while standard three-flavor mixing confines the expected flavor composition at Earth to a remarkably narrow and rigid band, high-energy modifications to the mixing parameters can break this restriction, driving the flavor composition into expansive new regions accessible to future experiments.
III.1
Standard flavor transitions
A neutrino of a given flavor,
ν
α
\nu_{\alpha}
, is a superposition of mass eigenstates
ν
i
\nu_{i}
,
i.e.
,
ν
α
=
∑
i
=
1
3
U
α
​
i
∗
​
ν
i
,
\nu_{\alpha}=\sum_{i=1}^{3}U_{\alpha i}^{\ast}\nu_{i}\;,
(1)
where
U
α
​
i
U_{\alpha i}
are elements of the PMNS matrix. In its standard parametrization
[
113
]
, the matrix depends on three mixing angles (
θ
12
\theta_{12}
,
θ
23
\theta_{23}
,
θ
13
\theta_{13}
), and one CP-violation phase (
δ
CP
\delta_{\rm CP}
), whose values are determined experimentally.
Neutrinos
oscillate
: while propagating, the probability of detecting them as having a certain flavor, including one different from their original one, varies periodically as a function of energy and distance traveled. The oscillation length of neutrinos of energy
E
ν
E_{\nu}
is
4
​
π
​
E
ν
/
Δ
​
m
2
4\pi E_{\nu}/\Delta m^{2}
, where
Δ
​
m
2
≈
\Delta m^{2}\approx
10
-4
–10
-3
eV
2
is the squared-mass difference between the mass eigenstates. For high-energy astrophysical neutrinos with
E
ν
≳
1
E_{\nu}\gtrsim 1
TeV, the oscillation length is tiny compared to their cosmological-scale baselines of hundreds to Mpc to a few Gpc to Earth. Further, over these vast distances, the different mass-eigenstate wave packets physically separate due to their different group velocities, leading to a complete loss of coherence. This, combined with the spread in baselines due to the distribution of astrophysical sources and the limited energy resolution of neutrino telescopes, precludes sensitivity to the rapid oscillation in the flavor-transition probabilities.
Instead, we are sensitive to the average probabilities. The standard probability of a neutrino produced with flavor
α
\alpha
being detected with flavor
β
\beta
is
P
α
​
β
std
=
∑
i
=
1
3
|
U
α
​
i
|
2
​
|
U
β
​
i
|
2
.
P_{\alpha\beta}^{\mathrm{std}}=\sum_{i=1}^{3}|U_{\alpha i}|^{2}|U_{\beta i}|^{2}\;.
(2)
This standard formulation implicitly assumes that the mixing matrix at the source is identical to the mixing matrix at the detector. The values of the mixing parameters are determined by global fits to data from oscillation experiments with sub-TeV neutrino energies
[
3
,
4
,
5
]
.
III.2
Flavor transitions with modified high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters
Because neutrino production occurs primarily via pion decay (Sec.
III.3
), the momentum scale accessible at production is governed by the mass of the pion (
i.e.
,
Q
=
m
π
≈
140
Q=m_{\pi}\approx 140
MeV). Hence, the mixing parameters at production are well-approximated—as in Sec.
III.1
—by the standard mixing parameters measured in conventional sub-TeV oscillation experiments.
However, high-energy astrophysical neutrinos detected at Earth typically interact via deep inelastic scattering on nucleons (
ν
​
N
\nu N
DIS), where the
maximum kinematically allowed
transferred momentum can be significantly larger than in conventional oscillation experiments,
i.e.
,
Q
≈
Q\approx
TeV–PeV vs. GeV. (Although, as we explain below the
most likely
momenta for high-energy astrophysical neutrinos are in the 10–100 GeV range.) Thus, if the neutrino mixing parameters are subject to RG running, the mixing parameters at detection may show signs of this and significantly deviate from those at production.
To account for this effect, we introduce a distinct, modified detection-scale matrix
U
′
​
(
Q
)
U^{\prime}(Q)
parametrized by high-
Q
Q
mixing angles (
θ
12
′
,
θ
23
′
,
θ
13
′
\theta_{12}^{\prime},\theta_{23}^{\prime},\theta_{13}^{\prime}
) and a CP-violation phase (
δ
CP
′
\delta_{\mathrm{CP}}^{\prime}
). The mixing matrix at production,
U
U
, remains the PMNS matrix. Thus, the flavor-transition probability is modified to project the propagated mass eigenstates onto the altered high-
Q
Q
flavor basis at the detector,
i.e.
,
P
α
​
β
=
∑
i
=
1
3
|
U
α
​
i
|
2
​
|
U
β
​
i
′
|
2
.
P_{\alpha\beta}=\sum_{i=1}^{3}|U_{\alpha i}|^{2}|U^{\prime}_{\beta i}|^{2}\;.
(3)
Because
U
U
and
U
′
U^{\prime}
are unitary, the sum of probabilities equals unity,
i.e.
,
∑
α
P
α
​
β
=
1
\sum_{\alpha}P_{\alpha\beta}=1
. The goal of our work is to extract the values of the altered mixing parameters from the detection of high-energy astrophysical neutrinos.
The magnitude and direction of the changes to the mixing parameters depend on the value of
Q
Q
and on the specific RG running scenario adopted, of which there are multiple possibilities. Figure
1
shows one hand-picked example that introduces RG running via a generic dimension-6 operator in the SMEFT formalism, which we expand upon later, in Sec.
IV.5
.
An exhaustive exploration of the different viable RG running schemes that could affect high-energy astrophysical neutrinos is not the goal of our work. Instead, our goal is to assess the detectability of high-
Q
Q
modifications to neutrino mixing that apply to all possible RG running schemes, establishing realistic benchmarks for which schemes are experimentally testable. Later, we report on the minimum detectable size of these modifications. Our sweeping approach is motivated by the limited precision we find available in high-energy astrophysical neutrinos to test RG running, which suits model-independent searches better than model-dependent ones.
Thus, in the first part of our analysis we make the simplifying assumption that the mixing matrix at detection,
U
′
U^{\prime}
, while potentially different from
U
U
, is independent of
Q
Q
. In other words, we extract constant values of
θ
12
′
\theta_{12}^{\prime}
,
θ
23
′
\theta_{23}^{\prime}
,
θ
13
′
\theta_{13}^{\prime}
, and
δ
CP
′
\delta_{\rm CP}^{\prime}
, effectively representing their
Q
Q
-averaged values [formally defined later in Eq. (
34
)], without ascribing any specific RG running to them. In practice, we compute
P
α
​
β
P_{\alpha\beta}
in Eq. (
3
) by varying the modified mixing parameters away from their standard low-energy values. The standard parameters in
U
U
are fixed to their currently allowed values from the NuFIT 6.1 global analysis
[
5
]
, accounting for their experimental uncertainty.

. See Sec.

for details.
Figure

illustrates the dependence of the flavor-transition probabilities on the modified mixing parameters. We focus on the independent diagonal channels (
P

​

P_{ee}
,

) and the symmetric off-diagonal channels (
P

​

P_{e\mu}
,

P_{\mu\tau}
). The figure shows that modifications of the probabilities of up to tens of percent are possible, with different flavor-transition channels exhibiting distinct sensitivity to specific high-
Q
Q
mixing angles.
Broadly stated, large departures of
θ
12
′
\theta_{12}^{\prime}
,
θ
23
′
\theta_{23}^{\prime}
, and
θ
13
′
\theta_{13}^{\prime}
from their standard counterparts
θ
12
\theta_{12}
,
θ
23
\theta_{23}
, and
θ
13
\theta_{13}
can all impact the probabilities. Large values of
θ
13
′
\theta_{13}^{\prime}
have an especially strong effect given that its standard counterpart is small,
θ
13
≈
8
∘
\theta_{13}\approx 8^{\circ}
. Later, we find that the effect of varying
θ
12
′
\theta_{12}^{\prime}
is unfortunately obscured by the uncertainty on the flavor composition with which neutrinos are produced, mitigating our sensitivity to this angle. The impact of
δ
CP
′
\delta_{\rm CP}^{\prime}
remains as marginal as that of its standard counterpart,
δ
CP
\delta_{\rm CP}
. These observations prefigure the predominant sensitivity to
θ
23
′
\theta_{23}^{\prime}
and
θ
13
′
\theta_{13}^{\prime}
that we find later.
III.3
Producing high-energy neutrinos
In standard astrophysical scenarios, high-energy neutrinos are expected to be produced when high-energy protons—accelerated within astrophysical environments such as active galactic nuclei, gamma-ray bursts, or starburst galaxies—interact with ambient matter (
p
​
p
pp
interactions)
[
114
,
115
,
116
]
or radiation (
p
​
γ
p\gamma
interactions)
[
115
,
117
,
118
,
119
]
. These interactions produce charged and neutral pions. The neutral pions decay into gamma rays (
π
0
→
γ
​
γ
\pi^{0}\to\gamma\gamma
), while the charged pions decay to yield neutrinos.
The primary neutrino production channel is the decay of charged pions and their daughter muons,
i.e.
,
π
+
→
μ
+
+
ν
μ
\pi^{+}\to\mu^{+}+\nu_{\mu}
followed by
μ
+
→
e
+
+
ν
e
+
ν
¯
μ
\mu^{+}\to e^{+}+\nu_{e}+\bar{\nu}_{\mu}
, and their charge-conjugated processes. At the astrophysical sources, before any oscillations occur, this full decay chain yields an initial flavor composition, defined as
(
f
e
,
f
μ
,
f
τ
)
S
(f_{e},f_{\mu},f_{\tau})_{\rm S}
, where
f
α
,
S
f_{\alpha,\rm S}
is the proportion of
ν
α
+
ν
¯
α
\nu_{\alpha}+\bar{\nu}_{\alpha}
produced, of approximately
(
1
3
,
2
3
,
0
)
S
\left(\frac{1}{3},\frac{2}{3},0\right)_{\rm S}
. This is the nominal expectation for high-energy astrophysical neutrino production; we refer to it as the
full pion-decay
scenario.
However, the conditions at the source can significantly alter this initial composition. If the source harbors a strong magnetic field, the intermediate muons produced in pion decay may cool substantially via synchrotron radiation before they decay
[
39
,
120
,
49
]
. In this case, the high-energy neutrino flux is dominated by the initial pion decay, yielding a flavor composition of
(
0
,
1
,
0
)
S
(0,1,0)_{\rm S}
. This
muon-damped
scenario is expected to become dominant at the highest energies (see,
e.g.
, Refs.
[
53
,
58
]
), where the muon lifetime is sufficiently time-dilated to ensure severe energy losses prior to decay.
(A third, less common standard scenario arises from the beta-decay of free neutrons, which can be produced either from the photo-dissociation of accelerated heavy nuclei or from the
p
​
γ
p\gamma
interactions themselves
[
121
]
. The decay
n
→
p
+
e
−
+
ν
¯
e
n\to p+e^{-}+\bar{\nu}_{e}
yields a pure electron anti-neutrino flux, resulting in a flavor composition of
(
1
,
0
,
0
)
S
(1,0,0)_{\rm S}
. See Appendix
G.5.4
for why this scenario is less likely and why we do not consider it in our main results.)
Because of the high energy threshold required for tau-lepton production, standard astrophysical sources are expected to produce a negligible fraction of
ν
τ
\nu_{\tau}
[
122
]
. Thus, across all standard production mechanisms,
f
τ
,
S
≈
0

. While exotic scenarios—
e.g.
, decay of heavy dark matter
[
123
,
124
,
125
]
, interactions involving leptoquarks
[
126
]
, or sterile neutrinos
[
127
,
93
]
—can generate appreciable
f
τ
,
S
f_{\tau,\rm S}
, they lie outside the standard astrophysical paradigm. Consequently, the initial flavor composition of high-energy astrophysical neutrinos is generally bounded to mixtures of
ν
e
\nu_{e}
and
ν
μ
\nu_{\mu}
, parametrized as
(
f
e
,
S
,
1
−
f
e
,
S
,
0
)
S
(f_{e,{\rm S}},1-f_{e,{\rm S}},0)_{\rm S}
. Neutrino interactions with matter inside the sources are unlikely to modify these flavor ratios before exiting them
[
47
,
128
,
129
,
130
,
131
]
(see, however, Ref.
[
63
]
).

Regardless, we can draw physical insight from Fig.

1.
The variation of

. As

,
⊕

, the remaining flavor content split almost symmetrically between the muon and tau flavors, due to

≈

∘
\theta_{23}\approx 45^{\circ}
.
This results in a nearly linear trajectory in the flavor triangle pointing away from the pure-electron corner.
2.
The parameter

⁡

′

governs the

\theta_{23}
is close to maximal (
45
∘
45^{\circ}
), which leads to roughly equal fractions of
μ

and

\tau
flavors regardless of the initial composition. As seen in the sub-panels of Fig.

while enhancing

, with

f_{e,\oplus}
remaining largely decoupled. The flavor trajectory thus runs almost parallel to the
f

,
⊕
f_{\tau,\oplus}
axis. If next-generation neutrino telescopes measure a significant

asymmetry, it could be interpreted as evidence of RG evolution affecting the 2-3 sector.
3.

f_{e,\oplus}
with the other flavors beyond standard mixing. The phase,

, introduces only a mild modulation effect.
This behavior is present also for other choices of the flavor composition at the sources. Importantly, however, the magnitude of the effects varies depending on the choice. In particular, Fig.

, of up to 100%. We elaborate on why next.
III.5
Why is neutrino production via full pion decay less sensitive to RG running?
The relative robustness of the flavor ratios at Earth under the nominal expectation of production via full pion decay,
(
1
3
,
2
3
,
0
)
S
\left(\frac{1}{3},\frac{2}{3},0\right)_{\rm S}
, against high-
Q
Q
mixing-parameter variations, as compared to the muon-damped case,
(
0
:
1
:
0
)
S
(0:1:0)_{\rm S}
, originates from the unitarity of the mixing matrices and the averaging of different flavor-transition probabilities.
In the muon-damped scenario, the flavor ratios at Earth map exclusively to the muon transition probabilities,
f
β
,
⊕
=
P
μ
​
β
f_{\beta,\oplus}=P_{\mu\beta}
. Consequently, any high-
Q
Q
modification to the mixing angles is transferred undiluted to the flavor ratios at Earth. In contrast, the flavor composition at Earth under full pion-decay production is a weighted superposition:
f
β
,
⊕
=
1
3
​
P
e
​
β
+
2
3
​
P
μ
​
β
f_{\beta,\oplus}=\frac{1}{3}P_{e\beta}+\frac{2}{3}P_{\mu\beta}
. Because total probability is conserved (
i.e.
,
∑
α
P
α
​
β
=
1
\sum_{\alpha}P_{\alpha\beta}=1
), an RG-induced enhancement in one transition channel (
e.g.
,
P
μ
​
e
P_{\mu e}
) is typically accompanied by a suppression in another (
e.g.
,
P
e
​
e
P_{ee}
). When evaluating the electron flavor fraction at Earth,
f
e
,
⊕
=
1
3
​
P
e
​
e
+
2
3
​
P
μ
​
e
f_{e,\oplus}=\frac{1}{3}P_{ee}+\frac{2}{3}P_{\mu e}
, for instance, this inverse correlation leads to a partial cancellation of the parametric dependence. This weighted sum dilutes the impact of high-
Q
Q
modifications on the flavor ratios.
From a geometric perspective in flavor-composition space, this damping effect under full pion decay is a consequence of the proximity of its prediction at Earth to the flavor-democratic point of
(
1
3
,
1
3
,
1
3
)
⊕
\left(\frac{1}{3},\frac{1}{3},\frac{1}{3}\right)_{\oplus}
. Under standard mixing, where
θ
23
\theta_{23}
is nearly maximal and
θ
12
\theta_{12}
is large, the initial
(
1
3
,
2
3
,
0
)
S
\left(\frac{1}{3},\frac{2}{3},0\right)_{\rm S}
composition diffuses into a nearly flavor-democratic composition at Earth. Once a completely decohered quantum system reaches a highly mixed state, extreme deformations of the underlying dynamics governing its evolution are required to displace it significantly from equilibrium.
This principle generalizes broadly across astrophysical production mechanisms: sources with “purer” initial flavor composition evade this unitarity-driven dilution and provide superior sensitivity to high-energy mixing. For instance, a pure
ν
¯
e
\bar{\nu}_{e}
source driven by neutron decay,
(
1
,
0
,
0
)
S
(1,0,0)_{\rm S}
, yields
f
β
,
⊕
=
P
e
​
β
f_{\beta,\oplus}=P_{e\beta}
, exhibiting an extreme, unbuffered sensitivity to variations in
θ
12
′
\theta_{12}^{\prime}
that mirrors the sensitivity of the muon-damped case.
Later, when extracting the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters from flavor-composition measurements, our statistical procedure profiles over all possible flavor compositions at the sources, considering all of them as equally probable as a reflection of our extant ignorance on what is the real neutrino production mechanism. While some of these initial compositions provide high sensitivity to the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters, others do not. Our net sensitivity to the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters is thus the result of exploring across this entire space of possible flavor compositions at the sources.
III.6
Measuring the flavor composition
TeV–PeV neutrino detection.—
In water- and ice-based optical-Cherenkov high-energy neutrino telescopes
[
132
]
optimized for TeV–PeV neutrino detection, like IceCube
[
33
]
, KM3NeT
[
34
]
, and Baikal-GVD
[
35
]
neutrinos are detected via their deep inelastic scattering off nucleons (
ν
​
N
\nu N
DIS)
[
22
,
23
,
24
]
, which can be either charged-current (CC)—if mediated by a
W
W
boson—or neutral-current—if mediated by a
Z
Z
boson.
In CC interactions, the final state contains an energetic charged lepton of the same flavor as the incoming neutrino,
i.e.
, an electron, muon, or tau. In NC interactions, the final state lepton is instead a neutrino of the same flavor as the incoming one. In both cases, the final-state lepton receives, on average, 80% of the energy of the original neutrino. The remaining 20% is carried by the final-state hadrons created by the breaking-up and ensuing hadronization of the interacting nucleon.
In CC and NC interactions the final-state charged particles emit Cherenkov light, which propagates through the transparent medium of the detector (ice in IceCube, water in KM3NeT and Baikal-GVD), becoming attenuated in the process, and eventually being detected by an array of photomultipliers. From the spatial and time profiles of the detected light, analyses infer the energy, arrival direction, and flavor of the interacting neutrino.
Optical Cherenkov neutrino telescopes classify events into three primary topologies:
•
Cascades:
These are generated predominantly by the CC interactions of
ν
e
\nu_{e}
and
ν
τ
\nu_{\tau}
, which produce localized particle showers. NC interactions from all flavors also produce showers, but, because the NC cross section is smaller, and because a higher-energy, less abundant incident neutrino is required to produce an NC shower of the same deposited energy as a CC shower, the NC contribution is subdominant. However, because showers from CC
ν
e
\nu_{e}
, CC
ν
τ
\nu_{\tau}
, and NC interactions look nearly identical at a given energy, there is a degeneracy in measuring the
ν
e
\nu_{e}
and
ν
τ
\nu_{\tau}
flavor ratios.
•
Tracks:
These are primarily generated by CC
ν
μ
\nu_{\mu}
interactions, which produce an energetic final-state muon capable of traveling several kilometers, leaving a long, visible track of Cherenkov light alongside the hadronic shower at the interaction vertex. Tracks can also arise from CC
ν
τ
\nu_{\tau}
interactions when the final-state tau promptly decays into a muon (an
≈
18
%
\approx 18\%
branching ratio) and the production and decay showers cannot be spatially resolved.
•
Double cascades:
This topology is a unique signature of CC
ν
τ
\nu_{\tau}
interactions. It consists of a first shower from the initial neutrino-nucleon DIS, and, if the resulting tau is sufficiently energetic to travel a resolvable distance before decaying, a second shower from its subsequent decay.
Because of these inherent degeneracies—particularly between
ν
e
\nu_{e}
and
ν
τ
\nu_{\tau}
cascades—identifying the flavor of a neutrino on an event-by-event basis is effectively unfeasible.
Instead, the flavor fractions
f
⊕
,
α
f_{\oplus,\alpha}
are reconstructed collectively using a statistical ensemble of events. These analyses traditionally rely on “starting events”, where the neutrino interaction vertex occurs within the instrumented volume and all three event topologies can be distinguished. However, such analyses are statistically limited by the low event rate of astrophysical neutrinos (
e.g.
, about 10 neutrinos per
km
3
{\rm km}^{3}
per year above 60 TeV
[
133
]
). So far, flavor measurements have been carried out only on IceCube data, either by the IceCube Collaboration
[
134
,
135
,
136
,
137
,
112
]
or externally to it
[
138
,
139
,
54
]
.
To overcome these statistical limitations, flavor measurements can be significantly improved by including through-going tracks—events where a
ν
μ
\nu_{\mu}
interacts outside the instrumented volume, producing a muon that traverses the detector. While these events only constrain the
ν
μ
\nu_{\mu}
fraction, they are more numerous; when combined with starting events, they appreciably tighten the overall flavor composition fits, as shown in Ref.
[
135
]
.
Future analyses may mitigate the prevailing
ν
e
\nu_{e}
-
ν
τ
\nu_{\tau}
degeneracy by differentiating between
ν
e
\nu_{e}
-induced electromagnetic showers and the largely hadronic showers induced by
ν
τ
\nu_{\tau}
. This separation relies on late-time Cherenkov-light “echoes” from the decay of low-energy muons and the capture of neutrons
[
140
]
, the latter of which has shown promising preliminary results on IceCube data
[
141
,
142
]
(see Ref.
[
143
]
for earlier results). We do not, however, include echoes in our projections.
Present TeV–PeV flavor measurements.—
For the present flavor-composition measurements, we adopt the recent 11.4-year IceCube Medium Energy Starting Events (MESE) measurement
[
112
]
(Fig.
4
, right panel), the first reporting non-zero content of all flavors at 68% C.L. MESE events have energies from 1 TeV to 10 PeV and high astrophysical purity
[
144
,
145
]
. The right panel of Fig.
4
shows our approximation to this MESE measurement. The current measurement is broad enough to encompass both the standard mixing band and a significant fraction of the RG-allowed space, severely limiting our present ability to distinguish between the two regimes. However, projected combinations of the observations by multiple neutrino telescopes will boost sensitivity.
Future TeV–PeV flavor measurements.—
For our projected flavor-composition measurements by optical-Cherenkov neutrino telescopes, we adopt the same projections as Ref.
[
6
]
, based on multi-detector combinations. These are, in turn, based on the methods introduced in Ref.
[
146
]
to infer flavor-composition measurements from a combination of High-Energy Starting Events
[
147
,
33
,
148
,
149
,
150
,
133
]
(HESE, tracks, cascades, and double cascades above 60 TeV)—sensitive to all flavors—and through-going muons
[
151
,
152
]
. We forecast multi-telescope detection in existing IceCube, Baikal-GVD
[
35
]
, and KM3NeT
[
34
]
, plus in future telescopes
[
32
,
30
]
P-ONE
[
153
]
, IceCube-Gen2
[
154
]
, NEON
[
155
]
, TRIDENT
[
156
]
, and HUNT
[
157
]
—up to 30 times the size of IceCube—by scaling IceCube event rates by the detector size, as in Ref.
[
158
]
(also Refs.
[
41
,
159
,
62
,
146
,
158
]
). For details, see Refs.
[
146
,
6
]
.
The right panel of Fig.
4
shows our projections (at the 68% C.L.; for the full flavor likelihood, see the Suppl. Mat. of Ref.
[
6
]
). By 2040, combined multi-decade exposures from km
3
-scale detectors (IceCube, Baikal-GVD, and KM3NeT) will shrink the observational uncertainties dramatically. By 2050, the integration of tens-of-km
3
next-generation facilities (IceCube-Gen2, P-ONE, and HUNT) will yield precision contours capable of isolating specific source mechanisms.
Figure
4
shows the measurements are tightest on the muon flavor fraction,
f
μ
,
⊕
f_{\mu,\oplus}
, owing to the large number of through-going muon tracks present in the simulated data used to derive the measurements. The 2040 flavor-composition contour remains elongated along the approximate
f
e
,
⊕
f_{e,\oplus}
-
f
τ
,
⊕
f_{\tau,\oplus}
axis, reflecting the experimental difficulty in separating these two flavors in a HESE sample. By 2050, the innate tau-identification capabilities in the HESE sample—via double cascades—and immense event statistics shrink this contour into a tight, nearly symmetric region, constraining all three flavors comparably well.
The ultimate physical insight derived from these projections is clear: because the standard mixing prediction is so extraordinarily narrow, it constitutes an exceptionally pristine null hypothesis. If the observed high-statistics flavor composition ultimately converges outside this thin standard band, it will provide an astrophysics-independent signature of new high-energy dynamics affecting neutrino mixing.
Future UHE flavor measurements.—
In the UHE regime (
i.e.
, energies over 100 PeV), the rapidly decreasing astrophysical neutrino flux necessitates detection volumes vastly larger than what is feasible for optical-Cherenkov telescopes. Future UHE observatories
[
30
,
32
]
, like the planned radio array of IceCube-Gen2
[
154
]
, will overcome this (see,
e.g.
, Ref.
[
160
]
) by detecting the coherent radio-frequency emission—the Askaryan effect—produced by neutrino-induced particle showers propagating through the ice
[
161
,
162
]
. (Other techniques based on high-altitude fluorescence and Cherenkov-light detection are also being explored
[
163
,
164
]
.)
Reference
[
165
]
showed how flavor discrimination in radio detectors differs from that in optical telescopes. Because radio arrays observe the brief, intense radio flash emitted by the primary interaction rather than imaging kilometer-long tracks, they rely on the longitudinal development of the shower, which imprints itself on the angular distribution and frequency spectrum of the radio pulse. In CC
ν
e
\nu_{e}
interactions, the final-state electron from the DIS event initiates a purely electromagnetic shower. At UHE energies, the Landau-Pomeranchuk-Migdal (LPM) effect significantly elongates this electromagnetic cascade. Conversely, the identification of CC
ν
μ
\nu_{\mu}
and
ν
τ
\nu_{\tau}
interactions is achieved through their multiple catastrophic energy losses. As the high-energy final-state muon or tau propagates through the ice, it undergoes repeated, discrete energy losses that generate secondary showers, which can be detected as coincident radio pulses by multiple radio stations.
This stark difference in detection signatures allows radio arrays to cleanly separate CC
ν
e
\nu_{e}
interactions from the rest, effectively isolating the electron neutrino fraction,
f
e
,
⊕
f_{e,\oplus}
, from the combined non-electron fraction,
f
μ
,
⊕
+
f
τ
,
⊕
f_{\mu,\oplus}+f_{\tau,\oplus}
. For our projected UHE flavor-composition measurements, we adopt the sensitivities forecasted for the IceCube-Gen2 radio array from Ref.
[
165
]
. Because distinguishing
ν
μ
\nu_{\mu}
from
ν
τ
\nu_{\tau}
based on these multi-station catastrophic loss signatures is exceptionally challenging, the resulting UHE flavor contours are elongated along the
f
μ
,
⊕
f_{\mu,\oplus}
-
f
τ
,
⊕
f_{\tau,\oplus}
axis. Further, the lower neutrino flux expected at ultra-high energies (see Fig. 2 in Ref.
[
160
]
), compared to the flux at TeV–PeV energies, widens the UHE flavor contours compared to their TeV–PeV counterparts.
Despite this, the precision on
f
e
,
⊕
f_{e,\oplus}
provides a critical constraint. These projected UHE measurements will offer an independent, complementary probe of the flavor composition at the highest accessible energies. This is precisely the regime where new-physics effects—
e.g.
, Lorentz-invariance violation, novel neutrino interactions—are expected to grow with energy, providing an unprecedented test of the standard mixing paradigm. Below, we extract constraints on the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters from these UHE projections.
(The capabilities of the radio array of IceCube-Gen2 could be complemented by giant surface radio arrays like the Giant Radio Array for Neutrino Detection (GRAND)
[
166
]
. Reference
[
167
]
showed how combining the dedicated sensitivity of GRAND to Earth-skimming
ν
τ
\nu_{\tau}
in-air interactions
[
168
,
161
]
with all-flavor in-ice observations by IceCube-Gen2 radio array could break the
ν
μ
\nu_{\mu}
-
ν
τ
\nu_{\tau}
degeneracy, yielding tightened constraints on the UHE flavor composition. However, in our projections in this work we use exclusively the stand-alone flavor-measurement capabilities of the IceCube-Gen2 radio array described above.)

Flavor regions at Earth accessible by varying the high-

mixing parameters

To fully map the discovery potential of astrophysical flavor ratios, we must generalize beyond fixed benchmark choices of the flavor compositions the sources. While neutrino production mechanisms like full and muon-damped pion decay are well-motivated, astrophysical environments may produce arbitrary mixtures of
ν

\nu_{e}
and

.

Figure

RG-modified footprint encompasses the majority of the flavor space. This expansion confirms that RG running naturally accesses regions of flavor composition at Earth that are inaccessible by standard mixing.
Appendix

shows that the RG-allowed flavor region at Earth is spanned dominantly by the variation of

) in our projections assuming measurements based on neutrino production via full and muon-damped pion decay.
IV
Renormalization group running in neutrinos
We model the
Q
Q
-dependent evolution of neutrino mixing parameters, contrasting the negligible running in the SM with the potentially large, observable effects introduced by beyond-Standard-Model (BSM) frameworks like the MSSM and particularly dimension-6 operators in the SMEFT. By folding the momentum-dependent RGEs over the realistic
Q
Q
-distributions of deep inelastic scattering for TeV–PeV and UHE astrophysical neutrinos, we find that their steeply falling energy spectra heavily weight the interactions toward moderate momenta
Q
=
Q=
10–100 GeV, dampening the observable effects of the running. Individual SMEFT coefficients impact the mixing angles hierarchically:
θ
12
\theta_{12}
is uniquely sensitive due to its being governed by the small solar mass-squared splitting (
Δ
​
m
21
2
\Delta m^{2}_{21}
), while the running of
θ
13
\theta_{13}
and
θ
23
\theta_{23}
is suppressed by the larger atmospheric splitting (
Δ
​
m
31
2
\Delta m^{2}_{31}
).
IV.1
RG running of the neutrino mass matrix
So far, we have treated the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters as phenomenological quantities that we vary independently of each other. Now we turn to predicting their running from low to high
Q
Q
scales—from production to detection—explicitly. This is achieved by computing the RG running of the effective neutrino mass matrix between these two scales. Below, first we present an overview of RG running in neutrinos generically, and then we show examples within specific running schemes.
In the Standard Model (SM) extended with massive Majorana neutrinos, the masses and mixing parameters are generated after electroweak symmetry breaking via the unique dimension-5 Weinberg operator
[
7
]
,
ℒ
(
5
)
=
c
5
Λ
​
(

c
¯
​
H
~
)

(

~

​

)
+

,
\mathcal{L}^{(5)}=\frac{c_{5}}{\Lambda}(\overline{L^{c}}\tilde{H})(\tilde{H}^{T}L)+\text{h.c.}\;,
(5)
where
L
L
is the left-handed lepton doublet,
H
H
is the Higgs doublet, and
Λ
\Lambda
is the scale of new physics. After electroweak symmetry breaking, the Higgs field acquires a vacuum expectation value (VEV)
v
≈
246
v\approx 246
GeV, reducing this operator to the effective Majorana mass matrix for the active neutrinos,
M

=
−
[

2
/
(

​

)
]
​

c

is a symmetric matrix of dimensionless Wilson coefficients in flavor space. Diagonalizing the mass matrix using the PMNS mixing matrix,
U

, rotates the mass matrix into the mass basis, revealing the masses of the neutrino mass eigenstates
ν
1
\nu_{1}
,

, and

as its eigenvalues,

.
The evolution of the mass matrix with the momentum scale
Q

, is governed by a set of coupled non-linear RGEs. We restrict our analysis to one-loop RGEs. Two-loop corrections are suppressed by an additional loop factor of
1
/
(
16
​
π
2
)

and yield negligible shifts that fall well below the current experimental sensitivities for high-energy astrophysical neutrino flavor composition (Sec.

).
The generic one-loop RGE for

[

where

(

In Eq. (

α

Because these interactions do not distinguish between lepton generations, they scale the entire mass matrix uniformly and only affect the absolute neutrino mass scale, not the mixing. Thus,
α

Δ
​

In contrast, the matrix

†
​

m

m

≪
m

P

breaks the flavor symmetry. It is precisely this non-universal
P

The shape of

depends on the RG running scheme we adopt. While our first set of results on the high-

mixing parameters (

,

, and

),

Thus, below we motivate our study of the RG running of the neutrino mixing parameters by presenting three illustrative schemes: the SM, the MSSM, and the aforementioned dimension-6 SMEFT.
Regardless of the specific high-energy framework adopted, integrating the RGEs requires fixing boundary conditions on the mixing parameters. In all subsequent numerical evaluations, we anchor the initial neutrino mixing angles (
θ
12

,
θ

\theta_{23}

) at the reference scale
Q

=
1

GeV to the central best-fit values provided by the NuFIT 6.1 global fit. Because these NuFIT parameters are derived from comprehensive fits to sub-TeV neutrino oscillation data, using them as our low-
Q

boundary conditions ensures that the RG-modified flavor transitions we compute later for TeV–PeV astrophysical neutrinos remain strictly consistent with current constraints.

IV.3
RG running in the Standard Model
In the SM, the flavor-universal trace is

The flavor-dependent matrix in Eq. (

When

4

Figure
5
shows the SM RG running of the mixing parameters. It reveals that, even above
m

, the SM running is inherently constrained by the smallness of the tau Yukawa coupling. Consequently, for a normal neutrino mass ordering with a strictly hierarchical spectrum (

m
3

), the resulting SM running of the mixing angles across the entire 0.1–100 GeV range is negligible. In contrast, the RG running of the neutrino masses can be significant. This is because it is driven by

\alpha_{\rm SM}
in Eq. (

), which is dominated by the top-quark Yukawa coupling (

=

y_{t}=1
), roughly a hundred times higher than the tau Yukawa coupling.
IV.4
RG running in the MSSM
To induce large RG running in the neutrino mixing angles between low-
Q
Q
and high-
Q
Q
scales, we must consider new-physics frameworks. We consider first the MSSM.
Unlike the SM, the MSSM
[
10
,
11
]
requires two Higgs doublets. The doublet
H
u
H_{u}
, with VEV
v
u
v_{u}
, couples to up-type quarks, while the doublet
H
d
H_{d}
, with VEV
v
d
v_{d}
, couples to down-type quarks and charged leptons. Because the total electroweak VEV
v
v
is shared such that
v
d
=
v
​
cos
⁡
β
v_{d}=v\cos\beta
, where
tan
⁡
β
≡
v
u
/
v
d
\tan\beta\equiv v_{u}/v_{d}
, the charged leptons acquire their masses from a suppressed VEV when
tan
⁡
β
\tan\beta
is large.
Consequently, to reproduce the observed physical lepton masses, the effective Yukawa couplings of the charged leptons (
ℓ
=
e
,
μ
,
τ

) must be significantly enhanced by a factor of
1
/
cos
⁡
β
1/\cos\beta
, yielding

ℓ
eff
≃
y
ℓ
​
1
+
tan
2
⁡
β
.
y_{\ell}^{\text{eff}}\simeq y_{\ell}\sqrt{1+\tan^{2}\beta}\;.
(14)
Above the supersymmetry-breaking scale
M
SUSY
M_{\text{SUSY}}
[of at least 1–10 TeV in light of bounds from the Large Hadron Collider (LHC)], the beta function for the effective neutrino mass matrix retains the same form as in the SM, Eq. (
6
). However, the loop coefficients
α

and
P

are modified by the presence of supersymmetric partners and the second Higgs doublet. When
Q
<
M
SUSY
Q<M_{\text{SUSY}}
, the heavy supersymmetric partners and the extended Higgs sector are integrated out. The beta function abruptly transitions back to its SM form, slowing down the running of the mixing angles back to its negligible SM regime.
Crucially for the running of the mixing angles, the flavor-dependent matrix
P
MSSM
≃
(
Y
e
eff
)
†
​
Y
e
eff
P_{\text{MSSM}}\simeq(Y_{e}^{\text{eff}})^{\dagger}Y_{e}^{\text{eff}}
absorbs the

⁡
β
\tan\beta
enhancement from Eq. (

). As a result, for large values of
tan
⁡
β
\tan\beta
(
e.g.
,
tan
⁡

∼

IV.5
RG running in the SMEFT
Beyond specific supersymmetric extensions of the SM, the RG evolution can be modified generically using SMEFT. In this framework, the effects of heavy new physics residing at a scale
Λ
≫
v
\Lambda\gg v
(where
v
v
is the electroweak scale) are parametrized by an infinite tower of higher-dimensional operators added to the SM Lagrangian
[
14
,
15
,
16
,
17
]
:
ℒ
SMEFT
=
ℒ
SM
+
∑
d
=
5
∞
1
Λ
n
−
4
​
ℒ
(
d
)
\mathcal{L}_{\rm SMEFT}=\mathcal{L}_{\rm SM}+\sum_{d=5}^{\infty}\frac{1}{\Lambda^{n-4}}\mathcal{L}^{(d)}
.
The lowest-order addition, the dimension-5 Weinberg operator [Eq. (
5
)], violates lepton number (
Δ
​
L
=
2
\Delta L=2
) and generates Majorana neutrino masses; however, its phenomenological imprint on flavor transitions is strictly kinematic, yielding standard oscillation phases that scale as
∼
Δ
​

2
​

/

\sim\Delta m^{2}L/E
, which strictly decohere and vanish for high-energy astrophysical neutrinos (Sec.
III.1
). Odd-dimensional operators (

=
5
,
7
,
…
d=5,7,\dots
) generically violate lepton or baryon number and are highly suppressed.
Conversely, dimension-6 operators (
ℒ
(
6
)
\mathcal{L}^{(6)}
)
[
14
,
171
]
represent the leading-order, lepton- and baryon-number-conserving corrections that provide non-standard interactions
[

,

,
174
]
and lepton-flavor-violating effects. Because their interaction cross sections scale as
∼
(

/
Λ
)
2

∼

/
Λ
2
\sim E/\Lambda^{2}

e.g.
, dimension-8), their effects are suppressed by an overwhelming factor of
𝒪
​
(
v
4
/

)

or
𝒪
​
(

/

. Assuming the underlying EFT expansion remains valid (
E
,
v
≪
Λ
E,v\ll\Lambda
), dimension-8 and higher operators are entirely negligible compared to dimension-6 ones. For this reason, our analysis focuses exclusively on dimension-6 SMEFT operators. Specifically, we use the Warsaw basis
[

]
, which provides a complete, non-redundant set of independent dimension-6 operators.

introduce flavor-violating interactions that drive the RG running of the mixing angles and the CP-violation phase. Below the tau-mass threshold, tau-specific dimension-6 operators are integrated out alongside the SM tau lepton, and the flavor-dependent running is handed over to the muon Yukawa coupling. However, owing to its smallness, the RG running of the mixing angles effectively ceases.
The profound contrast in the RG evolution between the MSSM and the SMEFT frameworks arises from four fundamental differences in the tensor structures and magnitudes of their respective beta functions:
1.
The underlying flavor structures dictate entirely different mixing dynamics: in the MSSM, the active driver
P
MSSM
∝
diag
​
(
0
,
y
μ
2
,
y
τ
2
)
P_{\rm MSSM}\propto\text{diag}(0,y_{\mu}^{2},y_{\tau}^{2})
is diagonal in the flavor basis. Consequently, the term
P
T
​
M
ν
+
M
ν
​
P
P^{T}M_{\nu}+M_{\nu}P
in the beta function solely scales existing matrix elements multiplicatively (
d
​
M
i
​
j
/
d
​
t
∝
M
i
​
j
dM_{ij}/dt\propto M_{ij}
); this preserves the existing flavor topology and remains subject to the kinematic suppression of the mass gaps
Δ
​
m
31
2
\Delta m_{31}^{2}
and
Δ
​
m
32
2
\Delta m_{32}^{2}
. Conversely, the SMEFT insertion matrix
C
H
​
ℓ
(
3
)
C_{H\ell}^{(3)}
is dense. The additive matrix multiplication
C
T
​
M
ν
+
M
ν
​
C
C^{T}M_{\nu}+M_{\nu}C
in Eq. (
15
) breaks lepton flavor conservation, generating cross-talk between lepton generations that overcomes the atmospheric mass-splitting suppression and forces all mixing angles to run appreciably.
2.
While the MSSM driver is strictly real and relies entirely on the low-energy boundary condition of
δ
CP
\delta_{\rm CP}
(manually set by us to the current global best fit) to evolve CP-violation effects, the
C
H
​
ℓ
(
3
)
C_{H\ell}^{(3)}
matrix introduces explicit imaginary components. These act as an independent source of CP violation, appreciably driving
δ
CP
\delta_{\rm CP}
away from its low-energy value.
3.
The bare magnitude of the SMEFT driver is significantly larger: even with a
tan
⁡
β
=
10
\tan\beta=10
enhancement, the dominant MSSM coupling remains modest [
(
y
τ
eff
)
2
≃
0.01
(y_{\tau}^{\rm eff})^{2}\simeq 0.01
], whereas our tuned SMEFT parameters are
𝒪
​
(
0.1
​
–
​
1.0
)
\mathcal{O}(0.1\text{--}1.0)
, providing enhancement that is orders-of-magnitude more powerful.
4.
This structural disparity fundamentally alters the trajectory of the mass-squared differences,
Δ
​
m
i
​
j
2
\Delta m_{ij}^{2}
. In the MSSM, the absolute mass running is dominated by the flavor-universal scalar trace (
α
SM
​
M
ν
\alpha_{\rm SM}M_{\nu}
), which scales all mass eigenvalues uniformly and largely preserves the hierarchical mass splittings. In contrast, in the SMEFT framework, the large, non-universal diagonal elements of
C
H
​
ℓ
(
3
)
C_{H\ell}^{(3)}
(
e.g.
,
C
11
≠
C
33
C_{11}\neq C_{33}
) impose distinct, flavor-dependent shifts on the individual mass eigenvalues, causing
Δ
​
m
i
​
j
2
\Delta m_{ij}^{2}
to splinter and diverge beyond their sub-TeV allowed ranges at high momenta.
In what follows, when presenting RG running results explicitly, we will focus exclusively on the SMEFT, representative of a more general framework than MSSM.
IV.6
Momentum distribution accessible with high-energy astrophysical neutrinos
In the
ν
​
N
\nu N
DIS by which high-energy astrophysical neutrinos are detected (Sec.

), the transferred momentum can be significantly larger than in conventional oscillation experiments,
i.e.
,

≈

TeV–PeV vs. GeV, as stated in Sec.

. Such values could induce significant RG running of the mixing parameters, as in Fig.

.
In reality, however, we do not have experimental access to the value of the momentum transferred in the detection of high-energy astrophysical neutrinos in high-energy neutrino telescopes. Instead, in any particular

​

E

Q

​

​

.
The top panel of Fig.

(also Figs.

𝒫

(

)

After building the

Q
Q
of tens of GeV, rather than by the TeV–PeV range at the tail of the distributions.
Thus, the steeply falling energy distribution of high-energy astrophysical neutrinos dampens—but does not preclude—their potential to probe large RG running of the mixing parameters.
In practice, then, we are sensitive to the

The impact of mass hierarchy.—
The structure of

To produce our numerical results below, we adopt the normal mass ordering and the illustrative value of

This places the detection of the SMEFT RG modifications out of reach of present flavor-composition measurements. However, we show later that it is within reach of our multi-detector flavor measurement projections.

. In fact, Fig.

. This illustrates why, in our SMEFT constraints below, we find null sensitivity when assuming a projected flavor measurement centered on the standard-mixing expectation from full pion decay and positive sensitivity when assuming one centered on muon-damped pion decay.
IV.9
SMEFT limitations and approximations
The SMEFT RG running in our analysis, while sufficiently general to capture the predominant physical features, relies on a few well-motivated approximations:
•
Frozen SM couplings:
The gauge couplings (
g
1
,
g
2
g_{1},g_{2}
), top Yukawa (
y
t
y_{t}
), and Higgs self-coupling (
λ
\lambda
) are treated as constant over our integration window (0.1–100 GeV). The running of these parameters induces only sub-leading, second-order corrections to the mixing angles (
e.g.
, Ref.
[
12
]
), which are negligible compared to the substantial uncertainties of current astrophysical measurements.
•
Kinematic decoupling in the
MS
¯
\overline{\text{MS}}
scheme:
Our numerical integration employs the mass-independent modified minimal subtraction (
MS
¯
\overline{\text{MS}}
) renormalization scheme. In it, heavy particles (
e.g.
, the tau lepton) are instantaneously integrated out via a step function at their mass threshold (
Q
=
m
τ
Q=m_{\tau}
), producing an abrupt “kink” in the RGE slopes rather than a smooth kinematic transition. While a formally exact EFT treatment requires finite one-loop threshold matching to smooth these boundaries, the unmatched step-function approximation is standard practice and sufficient for demonstrating appreciable running across wide
Q
Q
ranges.
•
Ultraviolet (UV) completion:
The dimension-6 Wilson coefficients are inserted as static spurions. While a complete one-loop SMEFT calculation would couple the running of
C
H
​
ℓ
(
3
)
C_{H\ell}^{(3)}
to the overall integration, a frozen insertion provides a proxy for the full physical mechanism. Physically, the effective dimensionless matrix used in our RGEs is related to the bare Wilson coefficients of the underlying high-energy theory via the standard EFT expansion,
C
H
​
ℓ
(
3
)
≃
C
bare
​
(
v
2
/
Λ
SMEFT
2
)
C_{H\ell}^{(3)}\simeq C_{\text{bare}}(v^{2}/\Lambda_{\rm SMEFT}^{2})
. Thus, generating effective couplings of
𝒪
​
(
0.1
)
\mathcal{O}(0.1)
at low new-physics scales (
Λ
SMEFT
≫
1
\Lambda_{\rm SMEFT}\gg 1
TeV), like we have tacitly done, implies bare Wilson coefficients that challenge standard perturbativity limits (
C
bare
≳
4
​
π
C_{\text{bare}}\gtrsim 4\pi
). Consequently, our chosen SMEFT matrix should be interpreted merely as a phenomenological proof of principle: it serves to demonstrate how the flavor-violating tensor structure of dimension-6 operators can bridge the low- and high-
Q
Q
scales, rather than representing a strictly perturbative UV-complete model (which would require,
e.g.
, introducing new
Z
′
Z^{\prime}
bosons or leptoquarks).
•
Electroweak matching:
We assume continuous running across the electroweak scale (
M
W
≈
80.4
M_{W}\approx 80.4
GeV). A rigorous treatment would entail matching SMEFT to the low-energy effective field theory by integrating out the
W
±
W^{\pm}
and
Z
Z
bosons and the top quark, generating finite boundary shifts in the effective mass matrix. However, neglecting these sub-percent precision threshold corrections is well-justified, as our primary objective is to establish the phenomenology of RG mixing in high-energy astrophysical neutrinos, whose associated experimental errors are significantly larger.
V
Statistical procedure
We establish the frequentist profile-likelihood procedure we use to extract constraints on the generic high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters and the SMEFT coefficients. Because simultaneous variations of all parameters result in destructive interference and degeneracies, meaningful experimental bounds must be derived by varying a single parameter or coefficient at a time. Crucially, we incorporate the unknown initial electron neutrino fraction (
f
e
,
S
f_{e,{\rm S}}
) as a nuisance parameter, ensuring that our resulting limits robustly reflect our underlying astrophysical ignorance, rather than artificially benefiting from assuming a perfectly known flavor composition at the sources.
To assess the sensitivity to the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters and the SMEFT coefficients, we assume that the measured flavor composition at Earth is centered on its standard-oscillation expectations from neutrino production via full or muon-damped pion decay (Fig.
4
, right panel).
We employ a frequentist profile-likelihood methodology, generalizing the standard procedure used to extract standard mixing parameters from high-energy flavor measurements introduced in Ref.
[
6
]
.
As stated in Sec.
III.6
, we base our present-day parameter constraints on the IceCube 11.4-year MESE flavor composition
[
112
]
. We base our projected parameter constraints on our simulated multi-detector measurements of flavor composition combining HESE and through-going muons. Since these simulated measurements are produced assuming flavor-composition expectations from standard mixing (
i.e.
, contours centered on the full or muon-damped pion-decay expectations),
our projected constraints represent limits on the parameter values, rather than discovery prospects
.
V.1
Constraints on high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters
To assess the sensitivity to the standard and high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters,
𝜽
\boldsymbol{\theta}
and
𝜽

, we use the

. In present and projected results, we let

[

A simultaneous, all-parameter fit lacks the sensitivity required to break the severe degeneracies between the high-
Q

parameters and between them and
f

,
S

. As detailed in Appendix

, when all parameters vary simultaneously, destructive interference washes out the extreme flavor topologies, rendering the allowed regions indistinguishable from standard mixing.
To overcome this and establish meaningful bounds, we make the deliberate methodological decision to exclusively
constrain a single high-

parameter at a time
,
η

(one of

). We achieve this by pinning the remaining high-

ν
\nu
,

Δ

(valid asymptotically given the large event statistics of our projections, with inferred constraints yielding conservative over-coverage) to find

Counterintuitively, these targeted single-parameter contours can extend further out than the all-parameter flavor contours in Fig.
4
(for a fixed C.L.). This is because the all-parameter scans heavily weight the multidimensional probability density of the flavor ratios toward the highly-mixed center of the flavor triangle, statistically starving the boundaries of the allowed flavor region. This centralization occurs because broadly sampling the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters effectively randomizes the high-
Q
Q
mixing matrix
U
′
U^{\prime}
, causing its squared elements to naturally average to
⟨
|

​

′
|

\langle|U^{\prime}_{\alpha i}|^{2}\rangle\approx 1/3
. Consequently, the flavor-transition probabilities
P
α
​
β
=
∑
i
|
U
α
​
i
|
2
​
|
U

​

|

[Eq. (

)] factorize into
1

​
∑
i
|
U
α
​
i
|
2

. At this point, the values of the low-

∑

|

i

=

), forcing the flavor composition at Earth closer to the equal-flavor mixture,
(

,
1
3
,
1

)

, regardless of the flavor composition at the sources. (However, rather than collapsing to a single point, the finite dimensionality of the parameter space ensures that statistical fluctuations maintain a non-zero variance around this isotropic mean, spreading the 99% C.L. probability mass over the finite area shown in Fig.
4
.)
In Appendix

, we generalize the above statistical procedure to report two-dimensional profiled likelihoods, in order to highlight any underlying experimental correlation between measured high-
Q

parameters. In Appendix

, we generalize our one-dimensional procedure to allow for

).
V.2

.
As with the high-

, we find no sensitivity to constrain all SMEFT coefficients simultaneously. Thus, we instead constrain a single coefficient at a time (one of
C
11
C_{11}
,
Re
​
(
C
12
)
\text{Re}(C_{12})
,
Im
​
(
C
12
)
\text{Im}(C_{12})
,

​
(
C
13
)
\text{Re}(C_{13})
,

​
(

)
\text{Im}(C_{13})
,
C
22
C_{22}
,

​
(
C
23
)
\text{Re}(C_{23})
,

​
(

\text{Im}(C_{23})
, or
C

C_{33}
), by setting all other coefficients to zero and profiling over all remaining nuisance parameters (standard mixing parameters and possibly
f
e
,
S
f_{e,{\rm S}}
). Using Wilks’ theorem, we report one-dimensional allowed confidence intervals for each coefficient, and two-dimensional profiled likelihoods of each coefficient vs.
f
e
,
S
f_{e,{\rm S}}
.
As in the case of the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters in Sec.
V.1
, this single-parameter profiling of the SMEFT coefficients yields allowed flavor regions that extend significantly further out than those obtained by varying all SMEFT coefficients simultaneously, which are detailed in Appendix
H
. Notably, this contrast between the single-parameter and all-parameter contours is much more pronounced for the SMEFT coefficients than for the generic high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters.
This amplification stems from two compounding effects. First, the higher dimensionality of the SMEFT parameter space (nine coefficients versus four high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters) exacerbates the statistical concentration of measure toward the center of the flavor triangle, rendering the fine-tuned parameter combinations required to reach extreme flavor configurations exceedingly rare in a global scan. Second, unlike the purely trigonometric mapping of the generic high-
Q
Q
scenario, the SMEFT flavor evolution is governed by non-linear RG equations. Simultaneously activating all SMEFT operators introduces competing effective potentials that destructively interfere, actively scrambling the eigenvectors of the effective neutrino mass matrix during its high-
Q
Q
evolution and driving the system toward isotropic flavor mixing. By isolating a single SMEFT coefficient, we eliminate both the multidimensional probability dilution and the dynamical interference, allowing the resulting contours to accurately trace the reach of each individual operator, from which our constraining power originates.

We present the constraints derived from our analysis. While present-day IceCube MESE data lack the precision to constrain high-
Q
Q
mixing or SMEFT coefficients, future TeV–PeV multi-detector networks (IceCube-Gen2, KM3NeT, Baikal-GVD, P-ONE, TRIDENT, NEON, HUNT) will be capable of placing meaningful bounds by 2040 and 2050, whereas UHE measurements yield drastically weaker bounds due to the larger uncertainties of radio-based flavor tagging. These TeV–PeV limits are highly dependent on the astrophysical source model: we find no sensitivity if neutrinos are produced via full pion decay due to intrinsic probability averaging, but we place
𝒪
​
(
1
)
\mathcal{O}(1)
limits on SMEFT coefficients (at
Λ
SMEFT
=
1
\Lambda_{\rm SMEFT}=1
TeV) if production occurs via muon-damped pion decay. Finally, we highlight that these constraints exhibit a strong parameter hierarchy—tightest for
θ
23
′
\theta_{23}^{\prime}
and
C
11
C_{11}
—and offer a unique, complementary astrophysical probe capable of testing new physics and potentially distinguishing between Dirac and Majorana neutrinos.
VI.1
Present constraints
We find no sensitivity to either the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters or the dimension-6 SMEFT coefficients in the present TeV–PeV flavor-composition measurements based on the IceCube 11.4-year MESE sample. This is due to the relatively large uncertainties associated with this measurement (Fig.
4
, right panel) and, in the case of the SMEFT coefficients, to the small-to-moderate size of the expected RG deviations induced by them (Figs.
7
,
8
). Our projections based on multi-detector combinations, however, reveal upcoming meaningful sensitivity.
VI.2
Projected constraints on high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters
Figure
11
and Table
1
show that measurements of the TeV–PeV neutrino flavor composition have the potential to constrain the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters. Figure
12
and Table
2
show that, in contrast, measurements of the UHE (
>
100
>100
PeV) neutrino flavor composition have limited constraining power. These results confirm our theory expectations from Sec.
III.7
, which we summarize in the salient points below.
Overall, our results show that
the TeV–PeV neutrino flavor composition is suitable to place constraints only on large deviations of the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters relative to their standard values, while UHE flavor measurements currently lack the resolution to do so.
Later (Sec.
VI.3
), we show how this translates to the specific case of constraining SMEFT coefficients.
Flavor composition does not provide precision constraints.—
Although our TeV–PeV projections show meaningful constraints on the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters, these constraints are wide, with
1
​
σ
1\sigma
relative uncertainties of 100–200% being commonplace, the exact value depending on the parameter, the assumed neutrino production mechanism, and the multi-detector combination used for the projection. In contrast, the present-day precision on the standard mixing parameters, from NuFIT 6.1, is
≲
3
%
\lesssim 3\%
for the mixing angles and 16% for
δ
CP
\delta_{\rm CP}
(at
1
​
σ
1\sigma
), with expected improvements to come
[
41
]
.
The reason behind our wide constraints is two-fold. The first reason is experimental,
i.e.
, the significant uncertainty with which neutrino telescopes infer the flavor composition—as illustrated in Fig.
4
, right panel—reflecting the inherent challenge in separating signals from neutrinos of different flavor (Sec.
III.6
). However, while this uncertainty remains overwhelmingly dominant for UHE neutrinos, it quickly becomes sub-dominant in our TeV–PeV multi-detector projections.
The second reason is theoretical; it exists today and survives into our projections, where it becomes the leading cause of our wide TeV–PeV constraints: our ignorance of the flavor composition at the sources. As detailed in Sec.
V.1
, this ignorance forces us to profile over the
ν
e
\nu_{e}
fraction at the sources,
f
e
,
S
f_{e,{\rm S}}
, when computing constraints on the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters, and therefore weakens our sensitivity to them.
This profiling is inevitable,
since the flavor composition at the sources is inferred
[
56
,
41
]
from the same observations that are used to constrain the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters. The co-dependence with
f
e
,
S
f_{e,{\rm S}}
is illustrated by the two-dimensional profiled likelihood of the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters vs.
f
e
,
S
f_{e,{\rm S}}
in Figs.
E1
and
E2
, which show significant correlation.
Assuming instead the flavor composition to be known, as in Refs.
[
25
,
18
,
26
]
, yields high-
Q
Q
deviations in the flavor composition that appear deceivingly large enough to be detectable. However, these prospects are overly optimistic, and must be tempered by the inescapable uncertainty on
f
e
,
S
f_{e,{\rm S}}
to be made realistic, upon which they should become comparable to our TeV–PeV results.
UHE vs. TeV–PeV constraints.—
Despite UHE neutrinos probing an energy regime roughly three orders of magnitude higher than their TeV–PeV counterparts, their ability to constrain the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters is drastically inferior.
As Fig.
12
and Table
2
demonstrate, 10 years of observation with the projected IceCube-Gen2 radio array leaves the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters almost entirely unconstrained. The projected
1
​
σ
1\sigma
and
2
​
σ
2\sigma
allowed regions span nearly the full physical parameter space, regardless of the assumed UHE neutrino flux model (stemming either from optimistic fits to Telescope Array cosmic-ray
[
179
]
or pessimistic fits to Auger cosmic-ray data
[
177
]
) or the neutrino production mechanism (full or muon-damped pion decay).
This severe lack of sensitivity stems directly from the formidable experimental challenges at EeV energies: the inherently low expected event statistics
[
180
,
160
]
and the extreme difficulty of performing precise flavor-tagging via radio-detection techniques
[
165
]
yield uncertainties in the measured flavor composition that are vastly larger than those at TeV–PeV energies. Consequently, while UHE neutrinos offer a slightly extended lever arm to constrain the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters, the severe degradation in flavor resolution completely overrides this theoretical advantage when attempting to constrain them directly in a model-independent way.
Differentiated parameter sensitivity.—
The different high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters are constrained to different degrees. As detailed in Appendix
D
, deviations of
θ
12
′
\theta_{12}^{\prime}
and
θ
23
′
\theta_{23}^{\prime}
from their standard values shift the region of allowed flavor composition at Earth away from its standard-oscillation expectation more than deviations of
θ
13
′
\theta_{13}^{\prime}
and
δ
CP
′
\delta_{\rm CP}^{\prime}
. We can understand this hierarchy through the analytical approximations derived in Appendix
G
.
Specifically, the leading-order expansion of the shifts in the flavor fractions at Earth induced by the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters [Eqs. (
G87
)–(
G89
)] exposes a strong, direct dependence on
θ
12
′
\theta_{12}^{\prime}
and
θ
23
′
\theta_{23}^{\prime}
, while remaining largely insensitive to small variations in
θ
13
′
\theta_{13}^{\prime}
and
δ
CP
′
\delta_{\rm CP}^{\prime}
. Figures
3
and
C1
show the same differentiated behavior. This hierarchy translates imperfectly into the differences in the relative precision with which these parameters are constrained in Fig.
11
and Table
1
. In the UHE regime, however, this theoretical hierarchy is entirely washed out by the dominant experimental uncertainties (see Fig.
12
).
The angle
θ
23
′
\theta_{23}^{\prime}
is the most tightly constrained high-
Q
Q
parameter, reaching a
1
​
σ
1\sigma
precision of about 33% in our most ambitious 2050 TeV–PeV projections (assuming neutrino production via muon-damped pion decay). The reason, as illuminated by Eqs. (
G87
)–(
G89
) in Appendix
G
, is that
θ
23
′
\theta_{23}^{\prime}
directly governs the breaking of the
ν
μ
\nu_{\mu}
–
ν
τ
\nu_{\tau}
symmetry. Consequently, variations in
θ
23
′
\theta_{23}^{\prime}
, together with
f
e
,
S
f_{e,{\rm S}}
, result in deviations in the flavor composition that move nearly orthogonally away from the center of our assumed flavor-composition measurement, as shown in Fig.
D1
.
In contrast, variations in
θ
12
′
\theta_{12}^{\prime}
predominantly scale the
ν
e
\nu_{e}
fraction at Earth via
P
e
​
e
P_{ee}
(see Fig.
2
). While these variations are also prominent, they are aligned with the major axis of our assumed ellipse-like flavor-composition measurement, as shown also in Fig.
D1
, rendering them relatively harder to constrain, especially when assuming flavor measurements centered on the standard-mixing full-pion-decay expectation (more on this below). Further, Figs.
E1
and
E2
reveal stronger correlation between
θ
12
′
\theta_{12}^{\prime}
and
f
e
,
S
f_{e,{\rm S}}
than between
θ
23
′
\theta_{23}^{\prime}
and
f
e
,
S
f_{e,{\rm S}}
, since the former governs the
ν
e
\nu_{e}
content of the flux more directly. As a result, the profiling over
f
e
,
S
f_{e,{\rm S}}
weakens the constraints on
θ
12
′
\theta_{12}^{\prime}
more than it does the constraints on
θ
23
′
\theta_{23}^{\prime}
.
Neutrino production via full pion decay yields weaker constraints.—
The constraints on the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters are weaker when the projected flavor-composition measurement is assumed to be entered on the expectation from full pion decay than when centered on the expectation from muon-damped pion decay. This is due to the latter case being more sensitive to changes in individual flavor-transition channels, as explained in detail in Sec.
III.5
and Appendix
G
.
Cf.
the sensitivity of the flavor ratios to the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters under each production channel in Figs.
3
and
C1
. The underlying reason, as presented in Appendix
G.5.2
, is that for full pion decay (
f
e
,
S
=
1
/
3
f_{e,{\rm S}}=1/3
) the
ν
1
\nu_{1}
and
ν
2
\nu_{2}
populations at the sources are too similar to one another, which dampens the effect of the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters—notably, of
θ
12
′
\theta_{12}^{\prime}
—on the flavor composition at Earth.
Concretely, Table
1
shows that under muon-damped pion decay the allowed
1
​
σ
1\sigma
intervals on the high-
Q
Q
mixing angles are 2–4 times narrower than under full pion decay, and the
3
​
σ
3\sigma
intervals do not span the full allowed physical range, unlike full pion decay. (For UHE neutrinos, as noted above, the constraints remain weak regardless of the production mechanism; see Table
2
.) Further, under the TeV–PeV full-pion-decay projections, there is little improvement in the relative precision with which the mixing parameters can be constrained over time.
Since, from theory, we expect full pion decay to be the nominal neutrino production mechanism, the above results have the unfortunate consequence of weakening our future prospects of constraining the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters. Nevertheless, muon-damped pion production represents a viable possibility, especially towards higher neutrino energies
[
41
]
, where muon synchrotron losses may become significant
[
53
,
58
]
, depending on the intensity of the magnetic fields harbored by the sources.
Existing TeV–PeV neutrino telescopes can provide meaningful constraints.—
Our projections show that even combining observations exclusively by existing km
3
-scale neutrino telescopes IceCube, Baikal-GVD, and KM3NeT, it may be possible to constrain the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters by 2040. The relative uncertainties on the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters in these projected constraints are large: 100–200% (at
1
​
σ
1\sigma
) on
θ
12
′
\theta_{12}^{\prime}
and
θ
23
′
\theta_{23}^{\prime}
, factor-9-to-30 uncertainties on
θ
13
′
\theta_{13}^{\prime}
, and 78% or null uncertainty on
δ
CP
′
\delta_{\rm CP}^{\prime}
assuming neutrino production via muon-damped or full pion decay, respectively. These uncertainties represent the baseline benchmarks for the predicted size that the high-
Q
Q
mixing parameters must have in order to be experimentally distinguishable from their standard-mixing counterparts. (Reference
[
6
]
presents a similar argument geared at the measurement of the standard mixing parameters.)
The limits of precision.—
While our most ambitious TeV–PeV projections—combining multi-km
3
telescopes by 2040 and adding a tens-of-km
3
telescope by 2050—show improvement in the parameter constraints, this improvement is moderate. Broadly stated, the
1
​
σ
1\sigma
constraints remain roughly at the same level they would reach by 2040 using only existing telescopes. In parallel,
3
​
σ
3\sigma
constraints become viable for parameters for which they previously were not. The reason for the slow improvement is the same as before: the need to profile over
f
e
,
S
f_{e,{\rm S}}
, which remains throughout even in our farthest projections. The one exception occurs for
θ
23
′
\theta_{23}^{\prime}
—our best constrained parameter (see above)—in our projections assuming neutrino production via muon-damped pion decay. In this case, there is a more appreciable improvement in the
1
​
σ
1\sigma
precision, evolving from more than 100% using only existing telescopes by 2040 to 33% combining all detectors by 2050.
Figure
1
summarizes our results on high-
Q
Q
mixing-parameter constraints. It contrasts the allowed regions dictated by current low-energy global fits with our projected measurements for 2040 and 2050. The figure illustrates the hierarchy of parameter sensitivity: projected TeV–PeV multi-detector measurements place meaningful bounds on
θ
23
′
\theta_{23}^{\prime}
and
θ
13
′
\theta_{13}^{\prime}
, whereas UHE radio-detection projections remain nearly entirely washed out due to the resolution limits of EeV flavor tagging. This divergence in sensitivity occurs despite both regimes probing remarkably similar kinematics, sharing largely overlapping 68% and 99%
Q
Q
-range containment regions with representative momentum transfers of
Q
≈
20
Q\approx 20
GeV (TeV–PeV) and
Q
≈
40
Q\approx 40
GeV (UHE), as explained above.
In summary, while high precision eludes us, the capacity of TeV–PeV flavor measurements to bound high-
Q
Q
mixing provides a robust, model-independent foundation for testing new-physics scenarios, to which we now turn.
